        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="f3c21f94773142c9b090549c5931a628">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '3458031e-6344-41b9-bbc5-7da1a4ff09fa'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d198944602" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Transition metal layered oxides (Na<sub>x</sub>TMO<sub>2</sub>), boasting a high theoretical specific capacity and affordability, have emerged as prominent cathodes for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). Their potential, however, is hindered when operating at higher voltage range (4.0–4.3&nbsp;V) due to irreversible phase transition, heterogeneous surface reconstruction, and side reaction. Herein, using a straightforward room-temperature liquid-phase reductive method, a dual conformal protective layer is in situ constructed on the surface of NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>(NFM). This layer comprises both a spinel structure and an amorphous Co<sub>x</sub>B coating, thereby achieving a layered-spinel-Co<sub>x</sub>B configuration. The spinel structure provides 3D Na<sup>+</sup> transport channels and works as a pillar to anchor the intrinsic layered structure. Simultaneously, the external Co<sub>x</sub>B layer effectively mitigates O loss, transition metal ion dissolution, and undesired side reactions on the surface. Benefiting from the synergistic effects on both the material's bulk and surface, the 1wt% Co<sub>x</sub>B coated cathode displays superior stability. After 300 cycles, the capacity retention is 79.6% between 2 and 4&nbsp;V, significantly outperforming pristine-NFM's(p-NFM) 51.4%. When charged to 4.3&nbsp;V, its capacity retention stands at 70%, much higher than that of p-NFM (51.2%). This work provides new insights into exploiting high-voltage stable cathode through constructing a dual conformal protective layer for high energy density SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202303773-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202303773-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), with abundant resources and low cost, have gained significant prominence as a prospective complement to Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) in the field of low-speed electric vehicles and energy storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0001_R_d198944593e423" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Energy Mater.">1</a>-<a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0003_R_d198944593e423" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Energy Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, due to the larger mass and higher redox potential of Na<sup>+</sup>, both the specific capacity and operating voltage of SIBs are lower than those of LIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0004_R_d198944593e430" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Joule">4</a>, <a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0005_R_d198944593e430" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Cathodes play a critical role in overcoming the energy density bottleneck of SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0006_R_d198944593e436" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Energy Storage Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among various varieties of cathodes, transition metal layered oxides (Na<sub>x</sub>TMO<sub>2</sub>) exhibit the highest theoretical specific capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0007_R_d198944593e445" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - RSC Adv.">7</a>, <a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0008_R_d198944593e445" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, driving the advancement of Na<sub>x</sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> cathodes with a robust reversible capacity is pivotal for enhancing the energy density of SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0009_R_d198944593e455" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Acc. Chem. Res.">9</a>, <a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0010_R_d198944593e455" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - ACS Energy Lett.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Na<sub>x</sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> primarily consists of O3-type and P2-type structures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0011_R_d198944593e466" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Phys. BC">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Within these, the O3-type Na<sub>x</sub>TMO<sub>2</sub>, owing to its higher sodium content (x&gt;0.7), demonstrates a superior reversible capacity when applied in full cells.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0012_R_d198944593e476" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Energy Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As a cobalt-free O3-Na<sub>x</sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> cathode, NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NFM) boasts the advantages of cost-effectiveness, environmental benignity, and a notably stable structure, positioning it as one of the most auspicious candidates for cathode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0013_R_d198944593e494" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Electrochem. Commun.">13</a>, <a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0014_R_d198944593e494" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, despite NFM's theoretical specific capacity reaching up to 240 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, its operating voltage is currently limited to &lt; 4.0&nbsp;V to ensure a longer cycling life due to high voltage instability, which can obtain a specific capacity in the range of 130–140 mAh g.<sup>−1[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0015_R_d198944593e502" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J. Power Sources">15</a>, <a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0016_R_d198944593e502" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Chem. Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The instability at elevated voltage levels (4.0–4.3&nbsp;V) mainly arises from two aspects<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0017_R_d198944593e507" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Energy Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>: 1) An irreversible phase transition from P3 to OP2 takes place, causing drastic lattice distortion and contraction. This transition leads to the recurrent formation of microcracks throughout the cycling process, ultimately resulting in structural deterioration and electrochemical performance degradation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0018_R_d198944593e512" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Power Sources">18</a>-<a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0020_R_d198944593e512" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Power Sources">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> 2) The cathode-electrolyte interface undergoes severer side reactions, subsequently triggering surface transition metal reduction, surface crystal lattice heterogeneous reconstruction, metal ions dissolution and concurrent oxygen loss.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0021_R_d198944593e518" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Energy Mater.">21</a>, <a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0022_R_d198944593e518" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - J. Mater. Chem. A">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The lost oxygen species further stimulate oxidation of the electrolyte, establishing a vicious circle. The irreversible phase transition in the bulk and degradation on the cathode interface result in poor cycling stability and Na<sup>+</sup> transport kinetics for NFM at high cutoff voltages.</p>
         
         <p>To date, various modification techniques, including morphology design,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0023_R_d198944593e528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> element doping,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0024_R_d198944593e533" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Energy Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> crystal structure modulation,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0025_R_d198944593e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and surface engineering,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0026_R_d198944593e544" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Mater. Chem. A">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> play significant roles in enhancing the cathode's bulk and interface stability. Among these, surface engineering can effectively mitigate the interface side reaction through the application of various coating. For instance, using the solid-state method or atomic layer deposition (ALD) to cover the cathode particles with metal oxides (Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0027_R_d198944593e554" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">27</a>, <a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0028_R_d198944593e554" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> TiO<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0029_R_d198944593e561" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">29</a>, <a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0030_R_d198944593e561" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> MgO,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0031_R_d198944593e566" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">31</a>, <a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0032_R_d198944593e566" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Mater. Chem. A">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> ZrO<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0033_R_d198944593e574" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Energy Storage Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> ZnO<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0034_R_d198944593e579" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> et&nbsp;al.) can prevent direct exposure of the cathode to the electrolyte, thus alleviating HF attack and active species dissolving. Silicate (Na<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>3</sub><sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0035_R_d198944593e588" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J. Power Sources">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>) or phosphate (Mg<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0036_R_d198944593e600" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Chem. Eng. J.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> AlPO<sub>4</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0037_R_d198944593e607" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - iScience">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0038_R_d198944593e619" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Chem. Eng. J.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> NaPO<sub>3</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0039_R_d198944593e627" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Energy Storage Mater.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na<sub>3-3x</sub>Al<sub>x</sub>PO<sub>4</sub><sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0040_R_d198944593e637" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - ACS Energy Lett.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>) coatings with higher Na<sup>+</sup> conductivity provide fast Na<sup>+</sup> transport channels as well as inhibit parasitic reactions. However, a single coating layer offers limited effectiveness in providing interface protection and does not directly improve bulk structural stability for the cathode. The spinel structure (Fd-3m) possesses superior stability and open 3D Na<sup>+</sup> transport pathways.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0041_R_d198944593e649" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Energy Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In situ surface reconstruction into a spinel-like lattice coherently matched with the layered cathode can not only enhance surface Na<sup>+</sup> transport kinetics but also serve as an anchor to stabilize the bulk layered structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0027_R_d198944593e657" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">27</a>, <a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0042_R_d198944593e657" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Nano Energy">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, using only spinel-like surface is insufficient to suppress the dissolution of transition metal ions. Therefore, we propose a bifunctional strategy that concurrently achieves surface reconstruction with a spinel structure and applies a protective coating, synergistically leveraging the benefits of both surface engineering techniques.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we employed a room-temperature liquid-phase reductive method to in situ construct a dual conformal protective layer on the NFM surface, consisting of both a spinel layer and an amorphous Co<sub>x</sub>B coating, thereby achieving a finely tuned surface structure with a layered-spinel-Co<sub>x</sub>B configuration, named as x wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM (x = 0.5, 1, 2, 5, 10). Co<sub>x</sub>B demonstrates exceptional corrosion resistance and mechanical robustness, allowing it to withstand electrolyte erosion and inhibit cathode particle cracking or fracturing. In addition, Co<sub>x</sub>B can transform into a fast ion conductor when interacting with Na<sup>+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0043_R_d198944593e678" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Nat. Energy">43</a>, <a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0044_R_d198944593e678" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The intermediate spinel layer is coherently matched with the bulk layered structure, promoting Na<sup>+</sup> transport while inhibiting the sliding of the layered structure during the Na<sup>+</sup> deintercalation process, thereby stabilizing the bulk phase structure. In situ and ex situ X-ray diffraction (XRD) techniques revealed that 1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM exhibited reduced lattice contraction at high cut-off voltages. Using high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) and high-angle annular dark field (HAADF), the refined surface structure of cycled 1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM was observed, demonstrating greater surface stability with fewer reconstruction and crack formations. Benefiting from the synergistic effects of surface engineering on both the bulk and surface, the 1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM cathode achieves a notable long-cycle stability enhancement. After 300 cycles, it retains 79.6% of its initial specific capacity at 1 C rate between 2 and 4&nbsp;V, significantly more than p-NFM (51.4%). Furthermore, at a cut-off voltage of 4.3&nbsp;V, it still holds 70% capacity retention (300th) at 2 C, markedly higher than p-NFM's 51.2%. Thus, the dual surface engineering strategy presents as a promising approach for transcending the performance limitations associated with Na<sub>x</sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> cathodes.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202303773-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202303773-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202303773-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202303773-sec-0030-title">2.1 Materials Characterization</h3>
            
            <p><b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202303773-fig-0001">1a</a> presents a schematic of the synthesis process for a series of samples, all the samples (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information) belong to O3-type layered structures (α-NaFeO<sub>2</sub>, space group: R-3m), while the intensity of the XRD diffraction peaks diminishes as the content of the amorphous Co<sub>x</sub>B increases. The precise chemical compositions of all samples were determined by ICP, as shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). The weight percents of Co<sub>x</sub>B in different samples are consistent with our expectation in an acceptable error range. Notably, 1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM exhibits the best electrochemical performance (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14,S15</a>, Supporting Information) and thus serves as our primary subject for subsequent discussions. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303773-fig-0001">1b</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information) display the XRD Rietveld refinements for 1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM and p-NFM, respectively. The Rietveld refinement data in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information) show no obvious differences in the crystal structure. HRTEM images in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information) for p-NFM reveal its pure layered structure. However, on the surface of the 1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM particles, a spinel-like structure forms, coherently matching with the bulk layered crystal configuration. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303773-fig-0001">1c,d</a> demonstrates a three-section structure including amorphous, spinel, and layered configurations. The Fast Fourier Transform (FFT) of region 1 is indicative of a layered structure along the &lt;110&gt; zone axis, whereas the near-surface FFT of Region 2 confirms a coexistence of spinel and layered structure. HRTEM for more regions are displayed in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4–S7</a> (Supporting Information), we discover that the formation of the spinel structure extends inward along the direction perpendicular to the (003) crystal plane, whereas the surface parallel to the (003) plane exhibits an amorphous-layered configuration without the presence of a spinel structure. The model for the three-section structure is illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303773-fig-0001">1a</a>. To further validate the formation of the spinel structure, electron energy loss spectrometer (EELS) analysis was conducted at various depths within the particles, as presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303773-fig-0001">1e</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information). Co exhibits a stronger signal on the particle surface. A negative shift in the Mn L3 peak on the surface indicates a reduction in valence state, while no significant peak shifts are observed for Ni and Fe. The difference in energy (Δ<i>E</i>) between the O pre- and the K-edge is influenced by the hybridization of TM 3D and O 2p orbitals. Lower Δ<i>E</i> values at the surface indicate reduced hybridization, which is attributable to the reduction of transition metals and the loss of oxygen.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0021_R_d198944593e772" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Energy Mater.">21</a>, <a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0045_R_d198944593e772" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Green Energy Environ.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In a spinel structure, Mn always has a lower valence than in a layered structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0046_R_d198944593e778" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - ChemElectroChem">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, we can confirm the formation of the spinel structure. We believe that the formation process of the spinel phase primarily involves two steps. First, on the cathode surface, Na<sup>+</sup>/H<sup>+</sup> exchange occurs along the (003) lattice plane, resulting in the formation of a Na-deficient layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0047_R_d198944593e787" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - J. Mater. Chem. A">47</a>, <a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0048_R_d198944593e787" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Adv. Energy Mater.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This reduction in Na content can be confirmed by the ICP results in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). Subsequently, the high-valent Mn undergoes reduction under the function of the reducing agent NaBH<sub>4</sub>, further restructuring the crystal lattice to form the spinel phase. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303773-fig-0001">1f</a> shows the morphology of the 1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM particles, the particle surface and gaps are enveloped by an amorphous Co<sub>x</sub>B, leading to a smoother appearance compared to p-NFM (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). Utilizing the high angle annular dark field-scanning transmission electron microscopy (HADDF-STEM, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303773-fig-0001">1g</a>), we can observe a thin film with lower contrast coated on the particles. The corresponding EDS mapping and line scan (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information) demonstrate Co enrichment in the outermost layer, which is consistent with the results of EELS mapping (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information) and XPS (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information). XPS analysis indicates that the cathode surface is primarily composed of Co and B elements. The fitting results suggest that Co and B exist mainly in the form of Co<sub>x</sub>B and its oxo-complex salt species.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0049_R_d198944593e824" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Adv. Energy Mater.">49</a>, <a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0050_R_d198944593e824" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Chem. Sci.">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This is attributed to Co<sub>x</sub>B's nature as a metallic compound, spontaneously reacting with oxygen to yields stable compounds. However, this process is self-limiting in kinetics, likely due to the formation of a B<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>-like glassy interface layer that acts as a dense passivation layer,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0043_R_d198944593e835" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Nat. Energy">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which stabilizes the cathode's surface oxygen during electrochemical cycles. To sum up, a three-section structure composed of Co<sub>x</sub>B, spinel, and layered configuration forms on the particle surface of 1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM, which may play a vital role in enhancing stability and electrochemical performance.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202303773-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/074a18a8-12ea-4617-8b43-06d0c42914e6/aenm202303773-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/074a18a8-12ea-4617-8b43-06d0c42914e6/aenm202303773-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4f549d03-27c3-4f46-893b-b2a9bfb90808/aenm202303773-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/074a18a8-12ea-4617-8b43-06d0c42914e6/aenm202303773-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202303773-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202303773" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>

                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202303773-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202303773-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Performance Measurements</h3>
            
            <p>Electrochemical behaviors of different samples are examined in half-cells with sodium metal as the anode. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202303773-fig-0002">2a</a> shows the rate performances ranging from 0.2 to 5 C (1 C = 130 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) between 2 and 4&nbsp;V. 1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM exhibits improved rate performances for the open 3D Na<sup>+</sup> transport channels of spinel structure<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0027_R_d198944593e889" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">27</a>, <a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0051_R_d198944593e889" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - J. Alloys Compd.">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> on the modified surface, which can obtain a specific capacity of 99.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 C (80.8% of 0.2 C), higher than 89.97 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> of p-NFM (67.7% of 0.2 C). The first charge/discharge curves at a rate of 0.1 C between 2 and 4.3&nbsp;V of both samples are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303773-fig-0002">2b</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a> (Supporting Information). The initial charge and discharge specific capacities of 1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM are 187.8 and 158.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, with a coulombic efficiency of 84.5%, which are lower than those of p-NFM (194.8/168.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, coulombic efficiency: 86.7%). The lower initial coulombic efficiency of the 1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM may be due to the transition of Co<sub>x</sub>B into a fast ion conductor upon reaction with some Na ions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0044_R_d198944593e916" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The decrease of specific capacity is attributable to the typical capacity loss induced by inactive matter coating. With the surface engineering of Co<sub>x</sub>B and spinel structure, 1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM displays significant cycling stability at both low and high cut-off voltages. After 300 cycles at 1 C rate between 2 and 4&nbsp;V (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303773-fig-0002">2c</a>), 1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM retains 79.6% of its initial specific capacity, significantly outperforming p-NFM's 51.4%. Even at an elevated cut-off voltage of 4.3&nbsp;V (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303773-fig-0002">2d</a>), it sustains 70% of its initial specific capacity after 300 cycles at 2 C, which is substantially higher than p-NFM (51.2%). In Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15c</a> (Supporting Information), the average working voltage attenuation (discharge) of 1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM has been remarkably alleviated. The voltage decay for every cycle is 0.797&nbsp;mV, showing a 50% slower rate compared to p-NFM. The crystalline 1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM heated at 400 °C has also been prepared to investigate its performances, but it is not as effective as the amorphous Co<sub>x</sub>B coating. The detailed discussion is provided in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a> (Supporting Information). Additionally, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303773-fig-0002">2e</a> presents the dQ/dV curves taken from different cycles. It's evident that the two peaks observed in the positive and negative scans align with the two plateaus in the charge–discharge profiles, indicative of O3-P3 (2.8–3&nbsp;V) and P3-OP2 (3.7–4.2&nbsp;V) phase transitions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0019_R_d198944593e950" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Chem. Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> While the reduction peak ≈ 2.6&nbsp;V corresponds to the O'3–O3 phase transition caused by the uneven distribution of charge at high rate, which gradually diminishes during the subsequent activation process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0052_R_d198944593e955" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Chem. Eng. J.">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Initial cycles for both samples display marked electrochemical polarization. However, in contrast to p-NFM, where the peaks rapidly shift and diminish, the 1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM maintains a relatively slower peak shift and a more overlapped peak profile in subsequent cycles, indicating lower electrochemical polarization and higher reversible charge retention. For further demonstration of the superior electrochemical properties of 1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM, we performed GITT measurements to quantify the apparent Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient at multiple charging stages during different number of cycle (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303773-fig-0002">2f,g</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information). During the first cycle, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients of 1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM are slightly higher than those of p-NFM. These coefficients increase when transitioning from an O3-type to a P3-type structure and subsequently decrease upon further transformation to an OP2-type structure with significant c-axis contraction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0018_R_d198944593e978" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Power Sources">18</a>, <a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0020_R_d198944593e978" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Power Sources">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Notably, 1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM undergoes a relieved decrease in Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients at the end of the charge (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303773-fig-0002">2f</a>), indicating a mitigating contraction of c-axis to be confirmed in the following section. After 100 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303773-fig-0002">2g</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information), 1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM maintains Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients that are nearly identical to those from the first cycle and are significantly higher than those of p-NFM. This underlines the superior stability of the bulk and interface phases in 1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM, thereby ensuring rapid Na<sup>+</sup> migration and cycling stability.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202303773-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0bcba823-6fac-41f8-bf4c-ab5514087953/aenm202303773-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/0bcba823-6fac-41f8-bf4c-ab5514087953/aenm202303773-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/77bb37dd-28df-4888-a657-beb1bf643953/aenm202303773-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0bcba823-6fac-41f8-bf4c-ab5514087953/aenm202303773-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

                     </div>

                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202303773-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202303773-sec-0050-title">2.3 Mechanism Investigation</h3>
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202303773-sec-0060">
               
               <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section3" id="aenm202303773-sec-0060-title">2.3.1 Phase Transition Investigation in the Bulk Structure</h4>
               
               <p>To gain further insights into the influence of surface engineering on the evolution of the bulk structure, we utilized in situ XRD, complemented by ex situ XRD, to examine the phase transitions during the initial charge–discharge process. In situ XRD was tested between 2 and 4&nbsp;V at a rate of 0.2 C as shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303773-fig-0003">3a,d</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a> (Supporting Information). During the charging process, for both samples, the peaks of (003), (006), (107), and (108) for O3 structure undergo a negative shift and the other peaks show a positive shift, indicating an expansion of c-axis and a contraction along a-axis. Then the O3 structure transforms to P3 structure, evidenced by the emergence of a new (015) peak at ≈ 45°.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0052_R_d198944593e1054" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Chem. Eng. J.">52</a>, <a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0053_R_d198944593e1054" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> During discharge, all the peaks go through a reversible movement and recover to the initial O3 structure. Given the inherent limitations of our in situ XRD cell, in situ tests beyond 4&nbsp;V are difficult to achieve. Therefore, we conducted ex situ XRD testing for cells charged beyond 4&nbsp;V. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303773-fig-0003">3b,c,e,f</a> are the XRD of electrode disc at different charge states, accompanied by the corresponding charge/discharge curves. At 4.15&nbsp;V, 1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM has transformed from P3 structure to OP2 structure with the appearance of (002), (010), and (103) peaks.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0019_R_d198944593e1065" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Chem. Mater.">19</a>, <a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0020_R_d198944593e1065" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Power Sources">20</a>, <a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0054_R_d198944593e1065" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As charging continues up to 4.3&nbsp;V, the (002) peak consistently moves to higher angles, signifying a continuous contraction of the lattice parameter c. However, p-NFM manifests a more notable deviation to higher angles, denoting an intensified compression in its lattice structure. Although both cathodes can recover to O3 structure after the first cycle, only 1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM retains the O3 phase structure after 200 cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information), while p-NFM undergoes irreversible transformations. This suggests that the alleviated lattice contraction allows the 1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM to maintain an intact structure during prolonged cycling, enhancing phase transition reversibility and cycling stability. Such enhancements can be attributed to the protective effects of surface engineering. On the one hand, the spinel phase acts as an anchor to suppress lattice sliding and contraction,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0027_R_d198944593e1078" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">27</a>, <a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0041_R_d198944593e1078" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Energy Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and on the other hand, Co<sub>x</sub>B inhibits interfacial side reactions and crack formation. Collectively, these synergistic effects strengthen the structural stability of the 1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM.</p>
               
               <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
                  <figure class="figure" id="aenm202303773-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4dbee2bb-2744-4f81-bb32-49d881f423b3/aenm202303773-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                           <source srcset="/cms/asset/4dbee2bb-2744-4f81-bb32-49d881f423b3/aenm202303773-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d27105a8-53ae-4846-b5be-ad3a9a922428/aenm202303773-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4dbee2bb-2744-4f81-bb32-49d881f423b3/aenm202303773-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                        <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202303773-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202303773" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                        </div>
                        <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">In situ and ex situ XRD tests for p-NFM and 1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM. 2D contour maps of in situ XRD of a) p-NFM and d) 1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM between 2.0 and 4.0&nbsp;V at 0.2 C. Ex situ XRD tests at different state of charge and corresponding charge/discharge curves of b,c) p-NFM and e,f) 1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM.</div>
                     </figcaption>
                  </figure>
               </section>
            </section>
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202303773-sec-0070">
               
               <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section3" id="aenm202303773-sec-0070-title">2.3.2 Evolution of Surface Structure after Cycling</h4>
               
               <p>Improvements in cycling stability are closely tied to the changes in the interfacial structure. Therefore, we carried out a comprehensive set of characterizations on electrodes after 200 cycles between 2 and 4.3&nbsp;V to investigate the influence of surface engineering on interfacial structural evolution. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202303773-fig-0004">4a–d</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a> (Supporting Information) are XPS depth profiling for the investigation of cathode electrolyte interphase (CEI). Both electrodes are covered by C═O and C─O species decomposed from PC solvent,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0028_R_d198944593e1131" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and the (CF<sub>x</sub>) belongs to PVDF. The peaks of Cl and F are the decomposition products of NaClO<sub>4</sub> and FEC, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0055_R_d198944593e1141" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Carbon">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As the etching time extends, the undetected lattice oxygen species (TM-O) on the surface starts to emerge. Though the bulk phase has a stronger Na auger peak, the peaks of CEI, formed by the decomposition of the electrolyte, are gradually reduced. In comparison, the intensities of the surface O, Cl 2p, and F 1s peaks of 1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM decrease more rapidly, and the proportion of lattice oxygen is higher, suggesting a thinner CEI formation on its surface, which ensures better Na<sup>+</sup> transport. A thinner CEI indicates the Co<sub>x</sub>B coating effectively inhibits the side reaction between the cathode surface and electrolyte.</p>
               
               <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
                  <figure class="figure" id="aenm202303773-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3a88bbcd-af45-427f-b60e-25704f4a504e/aenm202303773-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                           <source srcset="/cms/asset/3a88bbcd-af45-427f-b60e-25704f4a504e/aenm202303773-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ab383f0e-2630-4a38-8dea-c373c3434eee/aenm202303773-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3a88bbcd-af45-427f-b60e-25704f4a504e/aenm202303773-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                        <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202303773-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202303773" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                        </div>
                        <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Surface composition and structure characterizations after 200 cycles between 2 and 4.3&nbsp;V. XPS for CEI analysis: a) C 1s and b) O 2p for p-NFM. c) C 1s and d) O 2p for 1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM. TEM and HRTEM images of e,f) p-NFM particles and g,h) 1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM particles.</div>
                     </figcaption>
                  </figure>
               </section>
               <p>The particle morphology and surface structure of cycled cathodes have been observed by TEM and HRTEM. We did not detect the presence of CEI-like substances, which might be attributed to their instability, undergoing swift decomposition during the sample-preparation process and under electron beam exposure. As can be seen, deep and broad cracks are distributed on the surface of p-NFM particles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303773-fig-0004">4e</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21a</a>, Supporting Information), whereas the surface of 1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM only displays narrow and shallow cracks (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303773-fig-0004">4g</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21b</a>, Supporting Information). HRTEM image (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303773-fig-0004">4f</a>) shows severe lattice twisting and dislocations near the p-NFM cracks, whereas the lattice near the 1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM cracks remains well-structured (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303773-fig-0004">4h</a>). These results suggest that the strain on the surface of the 1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM particles has been relieved, and the formation of cracks is effectively suppressed.</p>
               
               <p>To further investigate the mechanism behind the deterioration of the surface structure of p-NFM particles and the enhanced structural stability of the 1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM particles after cycling, we employed HRTEM and HADDF-STEM for refined structural characterization, as illustrated in Figures <a href="#aenm202303773-fig-0005">5</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22,S23</a> (Supporting Information). For p-NFM, there is a serious surface reconstruction from layered structure to spinel structure. It is worth noting that this surface reconstruction is anisotropic, which is associated with the Na<sup>+</sup> migration path. For the layered structure, Na<sup>+</sup> follows a 2D migration path, specifically moving along the (003) crystal plane. As seen in Figures&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303773-fig-0005">5a</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a> (Supporting Information), on the surface parallel to the (003) crystal plane, the reconstructed spinel structure is relatively thin, measuring &lt; 10&nbsp;nm. In contrast, on the surface perpendicular to the (003) crystal plane, a much thicker spinel structure (&gt;20&nbsp;nm) forms (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303773-fig-0005">5b,c</a>). This suggests that the surface reconstruction process is likely facilitated by Na<sup>+</sup> deintercalation, thus more pronounced in regions experiencing dynamic Na<sup>+</sup> transport.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0056_R_d198944593e1238" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Nat. Commun.">56</a>, <a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0057_R_d198944593e1238" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Concurrently, we find that this surface reconstruction is accompanied by transition metal reduction and oxygen loss, as evidenced by atomic-scale EELS shown in Figures <a href="#aenm202303773-fig-0006">6a,b</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a> (Supporting Information). EELS analysis was conducted from the surface to the bulk within the particles. Both Mn L3 and Ni L3 peaks exhibit negative shifts, signaling a reduction in valence state, while no peak shift is observed for Fe. A much lower Δ<i>E</i> value at the surface indicates reduced hybridization between TM 3d and O 2p, which is attributable to the reduction of transition metal ions and the loss of oxygen. The dissolution of low-valence transition metal ions and the lost oxygen participate in the oxidation of the electrolyte and promote an acceleration in surface reconstruction, leading to the continuous deterioration of the interfacial structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0021_R_d198944593e1253" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Energy Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
               
               <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
                  <figure class="figure" id="aenm202303773-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c1f29420-0156-4755-9a92-38760ad6e7f6/aenm202303773-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                           <source srcset="/cms/asset/c1f29420-0156-4755-9a92-38760ad6e7f6/aenm202303773-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/633825be-5e72-4952-ab9e-e2ca0a5d55cc/aenm202303773-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c1f29420-0156-4755-9a92-38760ad6e7f6/aenm202303773-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                        <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202303773-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202303773" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                        </div>
                        <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Surface fine micro-structure characterization and schematic of structure model for both cathodes after 200 cycles between 2.0 and 4.3&nbsp;V. Surface fine micro-structure of p-NFM: a) HRTEM. b,c) Atom scale HADDF-STEM and related regions’ FFT. Surface fine micro-structure of 1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM: d) HRTEM. e,f) Atom scale HADDF-STEM and related regions’ FFT. g) ICP results of TM ions dissolution tests. h) Structure model for the surface of p-NFM particle and i) Structure model for the surface of 1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM.</div>
                     </figcaption>
                  </figure>
               </section>
               <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
                  <figure class="figure" id="aenm202303773-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f2bac3f2-2dd9-40ef-9033-94d829704fec/aenm202303773-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                           <source srcset="/cms/asset/f2bac3f2-2dd9-40ef-9033-94d829704fec/aenm202303773-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/fef5d03b-569d-43e2-af8c-a5c027a6bd62/aenm202303773-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f2bac3f2-2dd9-40ef-9033-94d829704fec/aenm202303773-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                        <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202303773-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202303773" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                        </div>
                        <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Atomic-level EELS characterization. HADDF-STEM image and corresponding EELS from the surface to the bulk structure in the red frame: a,b) p-NFM, c,d) 1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM.</div>
                     </figcaption>
                  </figure>
               </section>
               <p>As for 1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM, the surface parallel to the (003) crystal plane displays an intact layered structure (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303773-fig-0005">5d</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23a</a>, Supporting Information). Whereas the spinel structure can only be observed on the surface perpendicular to the (003) crystal plane (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303773-fig-0005">5e,f</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23c,d</a>, Supporting Information), which is consistent with the primary surface structure with slight propagation of the spinel structure. EELS analysis in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303773-fig-0006">6b</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25</a> (Supporting Information) demonstrates that only Mn L3 peak shows a slight negative shift with marginal decrease of Δ<i>E</i> on the surface. Moreover, EELS mappings in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25</a> (Supporting Information) suggest that the protective Co<sub>x</sub>B layer remains on the surface even after 200 cycles. The above results imply the transition metal ions reduction and O loss in 1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM are favorably mitigated, and the surface reconstruction has almost stopped progressing. Consequently, the transition metal ions dissolution is efficiently suppressed as illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303773-fig-0005">5g</a> analyzed by ICP, and both lattice twisting and crack formation are substantially minimized (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303773-fig-0004">4</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
               
               <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303773-fig-0005">5h,i</a> is the schematic illustration of the surface structure for p-NFM and 1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM during cycling. For p-NFM (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303773-fig-0005">5h</a>), when charged to a high voltage, uneven Na<sup>+</sup> distribution and oxygen loss occur.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0057_R_d198944593e1362" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These behaviors further induce the migration, reduction, and dissolution of transition metal ions, triggering the reconstruction and formation of a spinel structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0021_R_d198944593e1368" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Energy Mater.">21</a>, <a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0058_R_d198944593e1368" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Energy Environ. Sci.">58</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the lattice mismatch between this spinel structure and the layered structure makes it unstable and leads to further propagation into the bulk phase. In contrast, benefiting from surface engineering, the pre-constructed spinel structure on the 1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303773-fig-0005">5i</a>) particle surface matches well with the lattice of the layered structure, ensuring structural stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0027_R_d198944593e1379" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> During the charge–discharge process, this spinel structure acts as a pillar, anchoring and stabilizing the layered structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0059_R_d198944593e1384" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - Adv. Mater.">59</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the outermost Co<sub>x</sub>B layer provides synergistic protection. The loss of O, the dissolution of transition metal ions, and the side reactions with the electrolyte are effectively suppressed, ensuring a stable interface.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0043_R_d198944593e1391" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Nat. Energy">43</a>, <a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0044_R_d198944593e1391" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Through the synergistic effects of surface engineering on both the bulk and surface, the 1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM cathode achieves a notable long-cycle stability enhancement.</p>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202303773-sec-0080">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202303773-sec-0080-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In conclusion, the successful integration of a dual conformal coating layer, combining a spinel-like structure and Co<sub>x</sub>B, has been actualized on the NFM surface. The lattice-matched spinel structure provides open 3D Na<sup>+</sup> migration channels and anchors the intrinsic layered structure, inhibiting the lattice distortion and slabs sliding. Simultaneously, the Co<sub>x</sub>B plays a crucial role in mitigating the loss of O, the dissolution of transition metal ions, and the side reactions with the electrolyte. Owing to the protective dual surface coating layer, 1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM ensures consistent reversible phase transitions, interface stability and particle integrity over extended cycling, achieving unparalleled cycle stability. Our study offers valuable insights into the development of stable high-voltage SIBs, underlining the importance of dual conformal protective layers for high energy density batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202303773-sec-0090">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202303773-sec-0090-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202303773-sec-0100">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202303773-sec-0110">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202303773-sec-0120">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202303773-sec-0120-title"> Materials Synthesis</h4>
                  
                  <p>The pristine NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (p-NFM) was prepared through a spray-drying technique as described in previous work.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0052_R_d198944593e1444" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Chem. Eng. J.">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For the preparation of cathode coated by spinel-like structure and 1wt% Co<sub>x</sub>B, 0.05&nbsp;g Co(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> and 1&nbsp;g p-NFM powder were ultrasonically dispersed in the ethanol, followed by a 2-h N<sub>2</sub> purge to evacuate air from the solution and reactor. Thereafter, 0.0151&nbsp;g NaBH<sub>4</sub> dissolved in 10&nbsp;ml ethanol was gradually added into the reactor and reacted for another 2&nbsp;h. The whole process was conducted under magnetic stirring and a N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere. Finally, the sample was washed with ethanol by vacuum filtration for at least 3 times, followed by overnight vacuum drying at 120 °C to obtain the final product (1wt%-Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM). Cathodes with different coating amounts were prepared through the same procedure by regulating the concentrations of Co(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> and NaBH<sub>4</sub> added, all the products were instantaneously moved to the Ar-filled glove box for preservation.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202303773-sec-0130">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202303773-sec-0130-title"> Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>The compositions of various cathodes were precisely determined using inductively coupled plasma atomic emission spectrometry (ICP-AES). For the metal ion dissolution test, the battery was disassembled after cycling. Both the electrode and the sodium foil were rinsed with PC solvent. The separator was then soaked in the rinse solution, after which the solvent was evaporated. Deionized water was added for thorough soaking, and the supernatant was taken for ICP-AES testing. X-ray diffraction (XRD) measurements in the 2θ range of 10–110° were applied by the Bruker D8 advance X, employing Cu-Kα radiation (λ = 1.542 Å). The GSAS-II software<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303773-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303773-bib-0060_R_d198944593e1480" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - J. Appl. Crystallogr.">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> was used for obtaining the Rietveld refinement data. In situ XRD was tested in the 2θ range of 10–60°. The tests were conducted using CR-2025 coin cells, where the cathode shell was opened with an 8&nbsp;mm hole and sealed with Kapton film. The cathode was prepared using a dry method, with a composition of 80wt% cathode material, 10wt% Vapor Grown Carbon Fiber (VGCF) as the conductive carbon, and 10wt% Polytetrafluoroethylene (PTFE) as the binder. After thorough grinding, the mixture was rolled into sheets and then cut into 12&nbsp;mm disc for further use. The FEI F20 transmission electron microscope was employed for transmission electron microscopy (TEM) and high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) studies. Microstructural and chemical composition characterizations at the atomic scale were conducted with a Cs-corrected Hitachi HF5000 microscope. Dual energy dispersive spectroscopes (X-Max 100 LTE, Oxford, UK) and a Gatan Quantum 965 EELS are integrated into the microscope. The Verios G4 (FEI) scanning electron microscope (SEM) and the energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS, Oxford) were employed to observe the sample morphology and elemental distribution, respectively. X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS, Thermo Scientific Nexsa) was used to determine the composition and the element valence state in the cathodes.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202303773-sec-0140">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202303773-sec-0140-title"> Electrochemical Tests</h4>
                  
                  <p>For electrochemical tests, the p-NFM and Co<sub>x</sub>B@NFM samples were both applied to aluminum foil. The slurry consisted of active material, conductive carbon and poly(vinylidene difluoride) binder (6wt% in N-methyl-2-pyrrolidone) with a mass ratio of 8:1:1. Following desiccation at 70 °C in a vacuum overnight, the cathodes were cut into 12&nbsp;mm disc electrodes. The mass loading of active material ranged from 2 to 3&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. CR-2025 coin cells were then assembled in the glove box filled with argon. The anode was a 14&nbsp;mm sodium metal disc, and the separator was glass fiber (Whatman, GF/D). 1.0&nbsp;M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in propylene carbonate (PC) with 5% FEC was used as electrolyte. Electrochemical evaluations were tested at 25 °C. Galvanostatic charge–discharge measurements were performed at various current densities (1 C = 130&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>) using a Neware system. Galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) measurements were conducted with 10&nbsp;min charge/discharge (0.1 C), followed by a 1-h rest within a voltage range of 2–4.3&nbsp;V.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
